<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Biodiversity</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Variety and variabiity of life forms</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/220px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/330px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/440px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sampling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> collected during summer 2008 in Northern <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> mixed woods, near LaRonge is an example regarding the species diversity of fungi. In this photo, there are also leaf <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Biodiversity</b> generally refers to the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/variety" title="wikt:variety">variety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variability" title="Genetic variability">variability</a> of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP), biodiversity typically measures variation at the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-1">[1]</a></sup> Terrestrial biodiversity tends to be greater near the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> which seems to be the result of the warm <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_productivity" title="Primary productivity">primary productivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Biodiversity is not distributed evenly on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and is richest in the tropics. These tropical forest ecosystems cover less than 10 percent of earth's surface, and contain about 90 percent of the world's species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">Marine biodiversity</a> tends to be highest along coasts in the Western <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperature</a> is highest, and in the mid-latitudinal band in all oceans. There are <a href="/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity" title="Latitudinal gradients in species diversity">latitudinal gradients in species diversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Biodiversity generally tends to cluster in hotspots,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and has been increasing through time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> but will be likely to slow in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rapid <a href="/wiki/Environmental_change" title="Environmental change">environmental changes</a> typically cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CockellKoeberl2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CockellKoeberl2006-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> More than 99.9 percent of all species that ever lived on Earth, amounting to over five billion species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-13">[13]</a></sup>  are estimated to be <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-15">[15]</a></sup> Estimates on the number of Earth's current <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> range from 10 million to 14 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-16">[16]</a></sup> of which about 1.2 million have been documented and over 86 percent have not yet been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLoS-20110823_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-20110823-17">[17]</a></sup> More recently, in May 2016, scientists reported that 1 trillion species are estimated to be on Earth currently with only one-thousandth of one percent described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-18">[18]</a></sup> The total amount of related <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> on Earth is estimated at 5.0 x 10<sup>37</sup> and weighs 50 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150718-rn_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150718-rn-19">[19]</a></sup> In comparison, the total <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">mass</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> has been estimated to be as much as 4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes#Derived_units" title="Tonnes">TtC</a> (trillion tons of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGCI-2015_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGCI-2015-20">[20]</a></sup> In July 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Universal_Common_Ancestor" title="Last Universal Common Ancestor">Last Universal Common Ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> living on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160725_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160725-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a> is about 4.54 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1997_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The earliest undisputed evidence of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a> dates at least from 3.5 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-27">[27]</a></sup> during the <a href="/wiki/Eoarchean" title="Eoarchean">Eoarchean</a> Era after a geological <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> started to solidify following the earlier molten <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean</a> Eon. There are <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> discovered in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TG-20131113-JP_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-TG-20131113-JP-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-30">[30]</a></sup> Other early physical evidence of a <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic substance</a> is <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">meta-sedimentary rocks</a> discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-31">[31]</a></sup> More recently, in 2015, "remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic life</a>" were found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> According to one of the researchers, "If life arose relatively quickly on Earth .. then it could be common in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">life began on Earth</a>, five major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinctions" title="Mass extinctions">mass extinctions</a> and several minor events have led to large and sudden drops in biodiversity. The <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> eon (the last 540 million years) marked a rapid growth in biodiversity via the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>—a period during which the majority of <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> first appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The next 400 million years included repeated, massive <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity losses</a> classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> events. In the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainforest_collapse" title="Rainforest collapse">rainforest collapse</a> led to a great loss of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-35">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, 251 million years ago, was the worst; vertebrate recovery took 30 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-36">[36]</a></sup> The most recent, the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, occurred 65 million years ago and has often attracted more attention than others because it resulted in the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The period since the emergence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> has displayed an ongoing biodiversity reduction and an accompanying loss of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>. Named the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a>, the reduction is caused primarily by <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impacts</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a> destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Conversely, biodiversity positively impacts <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">human health</a> in a number of ways, although a few negative effects are studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SalaMeyerson2009_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-SalaMeyerson2009-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> designated 2011–2020 as the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Decade_on_Biodiversity" title="United Nations Decade on Biodiversity">United Nations Decade on Biodiversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#Services_with_mixed_evidence"><span class="tocnumber">5.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Services with mixed evidence</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term <i>biological diversity</i> was used first by wildlife scientist and conservationist Raymond F. Dasmann in the year 1968 lay book <i>A Different Kind of Country</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dasmann1968_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dasmann1968-41">[41]</a></sup> advocating conservation. The term was widely adopted only after more than a decade, when in the 1980s it came into common usage in science and environmental policy. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lovejoy" title="Thomas Lovejoy">Thomas Lovejoy</a>, in the foreword to the book <i>Conservation Biology</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConsBiol80_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConsBiol80-42">[42]</a></sup> introduced the term to the scientific community. Until then the term "natural diversity" was common, introduced by The Science Division of <a href="/wiki/The_Nature_Conservancy" title="The Nature Conservancy">The Nature Conservancy</a> in an important 1975 study, "The Preservation of Natural Diversity." By the early 1980s TNC's Science program and its head, Robert E. Jenkins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Lovejoy and other leading conservation scientists at the time in America advocated the use of the term "biological diversity".
</p><p>The term's contracted form <i>biodiversity</i> may have been coined by W.G. Rosen in 1985 while planning the 1986 <i>National Forum on Biological Diversity</i> organized by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a> (NRC). It first appeared in a publication in 1988 when <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiologist</a> <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> used it as the title of the <a href="/wiki/Proceedings" title="Proceedings">proceedings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonSciences1988_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-WilsonSciences1988-44">[44]</a></sup> of that forum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since this period the term has achieved widespread use among biologists, environmentalists, political leaders and concerned citizens.
</p><p>A similar term in the United States is <i>"natural heritage."</i> It pre-dates the others and is more accepted by the wider audience interested in conservation. Broader than biodiversity, it includes geology and landforms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Biodiversity" is most commonly used to replace the more clearly defined and long established terms, <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Species_richness" title="Species richness">species richness</a>. Biologists most often define biodiversity as the "totality of genes, species and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> of a region".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsson2001_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsson2001-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-48">[48]</a></sup> An advantage of this definition is that it seems to describe most circumstances and presents a unified view of the traditional types of biological variety previously identified:
</p>
<ul><li>taxonomic diversity (usually measured at the species diversity level)</li>
<li>ecological diversity (often viewed from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_diversity" title="Ecosystem diversity">ecosystem diversity</a>)</li>
<li>morphological diversity (which stems from <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular diversity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Functional_group_(ecology)" title="Functional group (ecology)">functional diversity</a> (which is a measure of the number of functionally disparate species within a population (e.g. different feeding mechanism, different motility, predator vs prey, etc.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>)</li></ul>
<p>This multilevel construct is consistent with Datman and Lovejoy. An explicit definition consistent with this interpretation was first given in a paper by Bruce A. Wilcox commissioned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature_and_Natural_Resources" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources">International Union for the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources</a> (IUCN) for the 1982 World National Parks Conference. Wilcox's definition was "Biological diversity is the variety of life forms...at all levels of biological systems (i.e., molecular, organismic, population, species and ecosystem)...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcox84_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilcox84-51">[51]</a></sup>
The 1992 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> defined "biological diversity" as "the variability among living organisms from all sources, including, 'inter alia', <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecoregion" title="Terrestrial ecoregion">terrestrial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">aquatic ecosystems</a> and the ecological complexes of which they are part: this includes diversity within species, between species and of ecosystems".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth1996_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth1996-52">[52]</a></sup> This definition is used in the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth1996_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth1996-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>One textbook's definition is "variation of life at all levels of biological organization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonSpicer2004_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-GastonSpicer2004-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biodiversity can be defined genetically as the diversity of alleles, genes and <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. They study processes such as <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_transfer" title="Gene transfer">gene transfer</a> that drive evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcox84_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilcox84-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Measuring diversity at one level in a group of organisms may not precisely correspond to diversity at other levels. However, <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapod</a> (terrestrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a>) taxonomic and ecological diversity shows a very close correlation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG/220px-Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG/330px-Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG/440px-Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swiss_National_Park_131.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forest" title="Temperate coniferous forest">conifer forest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Swiss Alps</a> (<a href="/wiki/Swiss_National_Park" title="Swiss National Park">National Park</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Biodiversity is not evenly distributed, rather it varies greatly across the globe as well as within regions. Among other factors, the diversity of all living things (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a>) depends on temperature, precipitation, altitude, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a>, geography and the presence of other species. The study of the spatial distribution of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>, species and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, is the science of <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>.
</p><p>Diversity consistently measures higher in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> and in other localized regions such as the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Floristic_Region" title="Cape Floristic Region">Cape Floristic Region</a> and lower in polar regions generally.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rain_forests" title="Rain forests">Rain forests</a> that have had wet climates for a long time, such as <a href="/wiki/Yasuni_National_Park" title="Yasuni National Park">Yasuní National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, have particularly high biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dotearth_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-dotearth-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journal.pone.0008767_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-journal.pone.0008767-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Terrestrial biodiversity is thought to be up to 25 times greater than ocean biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A new method used in 2011, put the total number of species on Earth at 8.7 million, of which 2.1 million were estimated to live in the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-58">[58]</a></sup> However, this estimate seems to under-represent the diversity of microorganisms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitudinal_gradients">Latitudinal gradients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Latitudinal gradients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity" title="Latitudinal gradients in species diversity">Latitudinal gradients in species diversity</a></div>
<p>Generally, there is an increase in biodiversity from the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">poles</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>. Thus localities at lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a> have more species than localities at higher latitudes. This is often referred to as the latitudinal gradient in species diversity. Several ecological mechanisms may contribute to the gradient, but the ultimate factor behind many of them is the greater mean temperature at the equator compared to that of the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoraRobertson2003_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoraRobertson2003-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though terrestrial biodiversity declines from the equator to the poles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> some studies claim that this characteristic is unverified in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystems" title="Aquatic ecosystems">aquatic ecosystems</a>, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystems" title="Marine ecosystems">marine ecosystems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The latitudinal distribution of parasites does not appear to follow this rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorandKrasnov2010_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorandKrasnov2010-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, an alternative hypothesis ("the <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> biodiversity") was proposed to explain the biodiversity latitudinal gradient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In this study, the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> pool size and the fractal nature of ecosystems were combined to clarify some general patterns of this gradient. This hypothesis considers <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moisture" title="Moisture">moisture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Net_primary_production" title="Net primary production">net primary production</a> (NPP) as the main variables of an ecosystem niche and as the axis of the ecological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypervolume" title="Hypervolume">hypervolume</a>. In this way, it is possible to build fractal hypervolumes, whose <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a> rises up to three moving towards the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hotspots">Hotspots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hotspots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">biodiversity hotspot</a> is a region with a high level of <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> species that have experienced great <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>  The term hotspot was introduced in 1988 by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Myers" title="Norman Myers">Norman Myers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKee2004_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKee2004-70">[70]</a></sup> While hotspots are spread all over the world, the majority are forest areas and most are located in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Forest" title="Atlantic Forest">Atlantic Forest</a> is considered one such hotspot, containing roughly 20,000 plant species, 1,350 vertebrates and millions of insects, about half of which occur nowhere else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The island of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and India are also particularly notable. <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> is characterized by high biodiversity, with the highest rate of species by area unit worldwide and it has the largest number of endemics (species that are not found naturally anywhere else) of any country. About 10% of the species of the Earth can be found in Colombia, including over 1,900 species of bird, more than in Europe and North America combined, Colombia has 10% of the world's mammals species, 14% of the amphibian species and 18% of the bird species of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Madagascar_dry_deciduous_forests" title="Madagascar dry deciduous forests">Madagascar dry deciduous forests</a> and lowland rainforests possess a high ratio of <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Since the island separated from mainland <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> 66 million years ago, many species and ecosystems have evolved independently.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s 17,000 islands cover 735,355 square miles (1,904,560 km<sup>2</sup>) and contain 10% of the world's <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, 12% of mammals and 17% of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>—along with nearly 240 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Many regions of high biodiversity and/or endemism arise from specialized <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> which require unusual adaptations, for example, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> environments in high <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern European</a> peat <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Accurately measuring differences in biodiversity can be difficult. <a href="/wiki/Selection_bias" title="Selection bias">Selection bias</a> amongst researchers may contribute to biased empirical research for modern estimates of biodiversity. In 1768, Rev. <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a> succinctly observed of his <a href="/wiki/Selborne" title="Selborne">Selborne, Hampshire</a> <i>"all nature is so full, that that district produces the most variety which is the most examined."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White1887_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-White1887-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="627" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/300px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/450px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/600px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apparent marine fossil diversity during the Phanerozoic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosing2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosing2010-76">[76]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Biodiversity is the result of 3.5 billion years of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">origin of life</a> has not been definitely established by science, however some evidence suggests that life may already have been well-established only a few hundred million years after the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formation of the Earth</a>. Until approximately 600 million years ago, all life consisted of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> – <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">single-celled</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protozoan" title="Protozoan">protozoans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>.
</p><p>The history of biodiversity during the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> (the last 540 million years), starts with rapid growth during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>—a period during which nearly every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylum_(biology)" title="Phylum (biology)">phylum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> first appeared. Over the next 400 million years or so, invertebrate diversity showed little overall trend and vertebrate diversity shows an overall exponential trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup> This dramatic rise in diversity was marked by periodic, massive losses of diversity classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup> A significant loss occurred when rainforests collapsed in the carboniferous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-35">[35]</a></sup> The worst was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian-Triassic extinction event">Permian-Triassic extinction event</a>, 251 million years ago. Vertebrates took 30 million years to recover from this event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a> suggests that the last few million years featured the greatest biodiversity in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup> However, not all scientists support this view, since there is uncertainty as to how strongly the fossil record is biased by the greater availability and preservation of recent <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geologic</a> sections. Some scientists believe that corrected for sampling artifacts, modern biodiversity may not be much different from biodiversity 300 million years ago.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> whereas others consider the fossil record reasonably reflective of the diversification of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup> Estimates of the present global macroscopic species diversity vary from 2 million to 100 million, with a best estimate of somewhere near 9 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-58">[58]</a></sup> the vast majority <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heywood_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-heywood-78">[78]</a></sup> Diversity appears to increase continually in the absence of natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_diversification">Evolutionary diversification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evolutionary diversification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The existence of a <i>global carrying capacity</i>, limiting the amount of life that can live at once, is debated, as is the question of whether such a limit would also cap the number of species. While records of life in the sea shows a <a href="/wiki/Logistic_function" title="Logistic function">logistic</a> pattern of growth, life on land (insects, plants and tetrapods) shows an <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponential</a> rise in diversity. As one author states, "Tetrapods have not yet invaded 64 per cent of potentially habitable modes and it could be that without human influence the ecological and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> diversity of tetrapods would continue to increase in an exponential fashion until most or all of the available ecospace is filled."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>It also appears that the diversity continue to increase over time, especially after mass extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, changes through the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> correlate much better with the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_growth" title="Hyperbolic growth">hyperbolic</a> model (widely used in <a href="/wiki/Population_biology" title="Population biology">population biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macrosociology" title="Macrosociology">macrosociology</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> biodiversity) than with exponential and logistic models. The latter models imply that changes in diversity are guided by a first-order <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> (more ancestors, more descendants) and/or a <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a> arising from resource limitation. Hyperbolic model implies a second-order positive feedback. The hyperbolic pattern of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> growth arises from a second-order positive feedback between the population size and the rate of technological growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18677962_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18677962-81">[81]</a></sup> The hyperbolic character of biodiversity growth can be similarly accounted for by a feedback between diversity and community structure complexity. The similarity between the curves of biodiversity and human population probably comes from the fact that both are derived from the interference of the hyperbolic trend with cyclical and <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a> dynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18677962_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18677962-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most biologists agree however that the period since human emergence is part of a new mass extinction, named the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction_event" title="Holocene extinction event">Holocene extinction event</a>, caused primarily by the impact humans are having on the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It has been argued that the present rate of extinction is sufficient to eliminate most species on the planet Earth within 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2002_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2002-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, in his <i>Biodiversity-related Niches Differentiation Theory</i>,  <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Cazzolla_Gatti" title="Roberto Cazzolla Gatti">Roberto Cazzolla Gatti</a> proposed that species themselves are the architects of biodiversity, by proportionally increasing the number of potentially available niches in a given ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>  This study led to the idea that biodiversity is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocatalytic" title="Autocatalytic">autocatalytic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>  An <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> of interdependent <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> can be, therefore, considered as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergent_properties" title="Emergent properties">emergent</a> <a href="/wiki/Autocatalytic_set" title="Autocatalytic set">autocatalytic set</a> (a self-sustaining network of mutually "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalytic" title="Catalytic">catalytic</a>" entities), where one (group of) species enables the existence of (i.e., creates niches for) other species. This view offers a possible answer to the fundamental question of why so many species can <a href="/wiki/Coexistence_theory" title="Coexistence theory">coexist</a> in the same ecosystem.
</p><p>New species are regularly discovered (on average between 5–10,000 new species each year, most of them <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>) and many, though discovered, are not yet classified (estimates are that nearly 90% of all <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are not yet classified).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heywood_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-heywood-78">[78]</a></sup> Most of the terrestrial diversity is found in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> and in general, land has more species than the ocean; some 8.7 million species may exists on Earth, of which some 2.1 million live in the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_services">Ecosystem services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ecosystem services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2468" data-file-width="2000" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Summer field in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (Hamois). The blue flowers are <i><a href="/wiki/Centaurea_cyanus" title="Centaurea cyanus">Centaurea cyanus</a></i> and the red are <i><a href="/wiki/Papaver_rhoeas" title="Papaver rhoeas">Papaver rhoeas</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_balance_of_evidence">The balance of evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The balance of evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"Ecosystem services are the suite of benefits that ecosystems provide to humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup>  The natural species, or biota, are the caretakers of all ecosystems. It is as if the natural world is an enormous bank account of capital assets capable of paying life sustaining dividends indefinitely, but only if the capital is maintained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>These services come in three flavors:
</p>
<ol><li>Provisioning services which involve the production of renewable resources (e.g.: food, wood, fresh water)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Regulating services which are those that lessen environmental change (e.g.: climate regulation, pest/disease control)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cultural services represent human value and enjoyment (e.g.: landscape aesthetics, cultural heritage, outdoor recreation and spiritual significance)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>There have been many claims about biodiversity's effect on these ecosystem services, especially provisioning and regulating services. After an exhaustive survey through peer-reviewed literature to evaluate 36 different claims about biodiversity's effect on ecosystem services, 14 of those claims have been validated, 6 demonstrate mixed support or are unsupported, 3 are incorrect and 13 lack enough evidence to draw definitive conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_enhanced">Services enhanced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Services enhanced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Provisioning_services">Provisioning services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Provisioning services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Greater species diversity 
</p>
<ul><li>of plants increases fodder yield (synthesis of 271 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants (i.e. diversity within a single species) increases overall <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yield</a> (synthesis of 575 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grain-yield-increase-in-cereal-variety-mixtures_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-grain-yield-increase-in-cereal-variety-mixtures-91">[91]</a></sup> Although another review of 100 experimental studies reports mixed evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>of trees increases overall <a href="/wiki/Wood_production" title="Wood production">wood production</a> (Synthesis of 53 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tree-growth-in-monocultures-and-mixed_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-tree-growth-in-monocultures-and-mixed-93">[93]</a></sup> However, there is not enough data to draw a conclusion about the effect of tree trait diversity on wood production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulating_services">Regulating services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regulating services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Greater species diversity
</p>
<ul><li>of fish increases the stability of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> yield (Synthesis of 8 observational studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>of natural pest enemies decreases herbivorous pest populations (Data from two separate reviews; Synthesis of 266 experimental and observational studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual-review-ecology-evolution-systematics-vol-40_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-annual-review-ecology-evolution-systematics-vol-40-94">[94]</a></sup> Synthesis of 18 observational studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-functional-richness-and-ecosystem-services-bird-pred_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-functional-richness-and-ecosystem-services-bird-pred-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birds-as-predators-in-trop-agroforestry_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-birds-as-predators-in-trop-agroforestry-96">[96]</a></sup> Although another review of 38 experimental studies found mixed support for this claim, suggesting that in cases where mutual intraguild predation occurs, a single predatory species is often more effective<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-influence-of-intraguild-predation_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-influence-of-intraguild-predation-97">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants decreases disease prevalence on plants (Synthesis of 107 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants increases resistance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_invasion" title="Plant invasion">plant invasion</a> (Data from two separate reviews; Synthesis of 105 experimental studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-98">[98]</a></sup> Synthesis of 15 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biotic-resistance-to-exotic-plant-invasions_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-biotic-resistance-to-exotic-plant-invasions-99">[99]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>of plants increases carbon sequestration, but note that this finding only relates to actual uptake of carbon dioxide and not long term storage, see below; Synthesis of 479 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>plants increases <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_nutrient" title="Soil nutrient">soil nutrient</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remineralization" title="Remineralization">remineralization</a> (Synthesis of 103 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants increases soil organic matter (Synthesis of 85 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-98">[98]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_with_mixed_evidence">Services with mixed evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Services with mixed evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Provisioning_services_2">Provisioning services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Provisioning services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>None to date</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulating_services_2">Regulating services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Regulating services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>Greater species diversity of plants may or may not decrease herbivorous pest populations. Data from two separate reviews suggest that greater diversity decreases pest populations (Synthesis of 40 observational studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organic-agriculture-promotes-evenness-and-natural-pest-control_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-organic-agriculture-promotes-evenness-and-natural-pest-control-100">[100]</a></sup> Synthesis of 100 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems-92">[92]</a></sup> One review found mixed evidence (Synthesis of 287 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vegetational-diversity-and-arthropod-population-response_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-vegetational-diversity-and-arthropod-population-response-101">[101]</a></sup>), while another found contrary evidence (Synthesis of 100 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-98">[98]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>Greater species diversity of animals may or may not decrease disease prevalence on those animals (Synthesis of 45 experimental and observational studies),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impacts-of-biodiversity-on-infectious-deseases_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-impacts-of-biodiversity-on-infectious-deseases-102">[102]</a></sup> although a 2013 study offers more support showing that biodiversity may in fact enhance disease resistance within animal communities, at least in amphibian frog ponds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Many more studies must be published in support of diversity to sway the balance of evidence will be such that we can draw a general rule on this service.</li>
<li>Greater species and trait diversity of plants may or may not increase long term carbon storage (Synthesis of 33 observational studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-6"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Greater pollinator diversity may or may not increase pollination (Synthesis of 7 observational studies),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-7"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup> but a publication from March 2013 suggests that increased native pollinator diversity enhances pollen deposition (although not necessarily fruit set as the authors would have you believe, for details explore their lengthy supplementary material).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wild-pollinators-enhance-fruit-set-of-crops_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-wild-pollinators-enhance-fruit-set-of-crops-104">[104]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_hindered">Services hindered</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Services hindered">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Provisioning_services_3">Provisioning services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Provisioning services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>Greater species diversity of plants reduces primary production (Synthesis of 7 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulating_services_3">Regulating services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regulating services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>greater genetic and species diversity of a number of organisms reduces freshwater purification (Synthesis of 8 experimental studies, although an attempt by the authors to investigate the effect of detritivore diversity on freshwater purification was unsuccessful due to a lack of available evidence (only 1 <a href="/wiki/Observational_study" title="Observational study">observational study</a> was found<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-8"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Provisioning_services_4">Provisioning services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Provisioning services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>Effect of species diversity of plants on biofuel yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 3 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-9"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of fish on fishery yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 4 experimental studies and 1 observational study)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-10"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulating_services_4">Regulating services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regulating services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>Effect of species diversity on the stability of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators did not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-11"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of plants on the stability of fodder yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-12"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of plants on the stability of crop yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 1 study)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-13"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> of plants on the stability of crop yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-14"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of diversity on the stability of wood production (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-15"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of multiple taxa on <a href="/wiki/Erosion_control" title="Erosion control">erosion control</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies – they did however find studies on the effect of species diversity and root biomass)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-16"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of diversity on <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Flood_regulation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Flood regulation (page does not exist)">flood regulation</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-17"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species and trait diversity of plants on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_moisture" title="Soil moisture">soil moisture</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_87-18"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-87">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other sources have reported somewhat conflicting results and in 1997 Robert Costanza and his colleagues reported the estimated global value of ecosystem services (not captured in traditional markets) at an average of $33 trillion annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_age" title="Stone age">stone age</a>, species loss has accelerated above the average basal rate, driven by human activity. Estimates of species losses are at a rate 100-10,000 times as fast as is typical in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan2005_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan2005-106">[106]</a></sup>
Biodiversity also affords many non-material benefits including spiritual and aesthetic values, knowledge systems and education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan2005_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan2005-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_biodiversity" title="Agricultural biodiversity">Agricultural biodiversity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="964" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/250px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/375px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/500px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a></div></div></div>
<p>Agricultural diversity can be divided into two categories: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">intraspecific diversity</a>, which includes the genetic variety within a single species, like the potato (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solanum_tuberosum" title="Solanum tuberosum">Solanum tuberosum</a></i>) that is composed of many different forms and types (e.g. in the U.S. they might compare russet potatoes with new potatoes or purple potatoes, all different, but all part of the same species, <i>S. tuberosum</i>).
</p><p>The other category of agricultural diversity is called <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">interspecific diversity</a> and refers to the number and types of different species. Thinking about this diversity we might note that many small vegetable farmers grow many different crops like potatoes and also carrots, peppers, lettuce etc.
</p><p>Agricultural diversity can also be divided by whether it is ‘planned’ diversity or ‘associated’ diversity. This is a functional classification that we impose and not an intrinsic feature of life or diversity. Planned diversity includes the crops which a farmer has encouraged, planted or raised (e.g. crops, covers, symbionts and livestock, among others), which can be contrasted with the associated diversity that arrives among the crops, uninvited (e.g. herbivores, weed species and pathogens, among others).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecology-of-agroecosystems_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecology-of-agroecosystems-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The control of associated biodiversity is one of the great agricultural challenges that farmers face. On <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> farms, the approach is generally to eradicate associated diversity using a suite of biologically destructive <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, mechanized tools and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">transgenic engineering techniques</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">rotate crops</a>. Although some <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polyculture</a> farmers use the same techniques, they also employ <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a> strategies as well as strategies that are more labor-intensive, but generally less dependent on capital, biotechnology and energy.
</p><p>Interspecific crop diversity is, in part, responsible for offering variety in what we eat. Intraspecific diversity, the variety of alleles within a single species, also offers us choice in our diets. If a crop fails in a monoculture, we rely on agricultural diversity to replant the land with something new. If a wheat crop is destroyed by a pest we may plant a hardier variety of wheat the next year, relying on intraspecific diversity. We may forgo wheat production in that area and plant a different species altogether, relying on interspecific diversity. Even an agricultural society which primarily grows monocultures, relies on biodiversity at some point.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish potato blight</a> of 1846 was a major factor in the deaths of one million people and the emigration of about two million. It was the result of planting only two potato varieties, both vulnerable to the blight, <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i>, which arrived in 1845<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecology-of-agroecosystems_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-ecology-of-agroecosystems-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>When <a href="/wiki/Rice_grassy_stunt_virus" title="Rice grassy stunt virus">rice grassy stunt virus</a> struck rice fields from Indonesia to India in the 1970s, 6,273 varieties were tested for resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-108">[108]</a></sup> Only one was resistant, an Indian variety and known to science only since 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-108">[108]</a></sup> This variety formed a hybrid with other varieties and is now widely grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-108">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coffee_rust" title="Coffee rust">Coffee rust</a> attacked coffee plantations in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and Central America in 1970. A resistant variety was found in Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The diseases are themselves a form of biodiversity.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">Monoculture</a> was a contributing factor to several agricultural disasters, including the European wine industry collapse in the late 19th century and the US <a href="/wiki/Southern_corn_leaf_blight" title="Southern corn leaf blight">southern corn leaf blight</a> epidemic of 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although about 80 percent of humans' food supply comes from just 20 kinds of plants,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> humans use at least 40,000 species.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Many people depend on these species for food, shelter and clothing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Earth's surviving biodiversity provides resources for increasing the range of food and other products suitable for human use, although the present extinction rate shrinks that potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2002_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2002-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_health">Human health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Human health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="1000" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/170px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/255px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/340px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The diverse forest canopy on <a href="/wiki/Barro_Colorado_Island" title="Barro Colorado Island">Barro Colorado Island</a>, Panama, yielded this display of different fruit</div></div></div>
<p>Biodiversity's relevance to human health is becoming an international political issue, as scientific evidence builds on the global health implications of biodiversity loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiversity1_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-biodiversity1-114">[114]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CorvalánHales2005_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-CorvalánHales2005-115">[115]</a></sup> This issue is closely linked with the issue of climate change,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> as many of the anticipated health risks of climate change are associated with changes in biodiversity (e.g. changes in populations and distribution of disease vectors, scarcity of fresh water, impacts on agricultural biodiversity and food resources etc.). This is because the species most likely to disappear are those that buffer against infectious disease transmission, while surviving species tend to be the ones that increase disease transmission, such as that of West Nile Virus, Lyme disease and Hantavirus, according to a study done co-authored by Felicia Keesing, an ecologist at Bard College and Drew Harvell, associate director for Environment of the <a href="/wiki/Atkinson_Center_for_a_Sustainable_Future" title="Atkinson Center for a Sustainable Future">Atkinson Center for a Sustainable Future</a> (ACSF) at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The growing demand and lack of drinkable water on the planet presents an additional challenge to the future of human health. Partly, the problem lies in the success of water suppliers to increase supplies and failure of groups promoting preservation of water resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bank2010_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bank2010-118">[118]</a></sup> While the distribution of clean water increases, in some parts of the world it remains unequal. According to the World Health Organisation (2018) only 71% of the global population used a safely managed drinking-water service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the health issues influenced by biodiversity include dietary health and nutrition security, infectious disease, medical science and medicinal resources, social and psychological health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Biodiversity is also known to have an important role in reducing disaster risk and in post-disaster relief and recovery efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
